On the treatment of sores.
I have already said in the division of the treatment of sores what is appropriate for the free man, and I will also say the treatment of those with more resources and medications according to their condition.
Therefore, I say that sores are differentiated according to what causes them and according to the place where they occur.
And the causes that often cause sores are such as injuries from stones, or incision with a knife or sword, or striking with a lance, fist, or arrow, or similar to these.
But sores that occur in the parts of the body are such as a sore on the head, neck, foot, abdomen, chest, and similar to these body parts.
I also recommend the treatment for some sores using this reasoning and rule for other sores.
And I begin with the simple sores of the head, as the treatment for complex sores will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
When a simple sore occurs on the head and is not a fracture, consider: if it was caused by a stone or something similar, and the wound has been sewn up but remains large and you fear that the patient may develop an abscess, then you should immediately lance the skull and drain the pus, without delay; and remove what blood comes out according to the amount of its strength, unless there is some other reason prohibiting lancing, especially if the blood that has come out from the sore is scant.
And the lancing should be performed on the opposite side of the affected area, and keep the patient from reopening the wound, drinking alcohol, and eating rich foods.
But if there is any reason for caution, then reduce the food and do not approach meat or wine.
Wait on the sore if an abscess forms on it, let it be infused with rose oil or vinegar in which is steeped.
But if you are sure of the abscess, wait on the sore if it is new and has not yet changed its powder that I recommend keeping close by.
And if the air has already changed it, wait on some salve until it is healed.
But if the sore is large or caused by incision of space and similar things, and the edges are not properly aligned, then align them with sutures as I recommend for their suturing.
But if the sore has exposed the bone and not blocked, but only a small suspension suture is present, then incise it and care for the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the skin, and order the patient to avoid rich foods: meat and fish and similar things, until it is healed.
And if there is rotting of the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, then incise everything possible and remove it, and care for it.
But if in these sores there is an artery or vein from which blood flow can occur and has not been separated by medicines, then you should explore the artery, and if it is not incised, incise it with a spatula or lance it; and if necessity requires that what I have said does not conform, then cauterize it until the blood is released.
But if these sores are simple and small, the cause of them is light; therefore, sprinkle this powder on them, as it will solidify before the air changes them.
The method for the powder is as follows: mix one part of oil from crushed walnuts, two parts of dragon's blood sap, three parts of non-extinct herbs; grind and sift everything, fill the sore with it, bind it tightly with a good binding until it adheres well, and let it coagulate with blood.
And strengthen it above with powder and let it dry, and do not remove it until it forms an abscess, as the medicine will not be effective until it is healed.
And sometimes do only cautery when you do not have walnut oil and dragon's blood sap.
And now perform this operation on large sores when it is necessary to wait on them and they are tightened, and only recent sores with their own blood, which the air has not yet altered.
And if the air has altered it, and especially in summer, do not consider the position of this powder, but instead care for them as I have said, placing on them various salves or powders made from barley flour with water and honey until they form scabs; and care for them with other treatment until they are healed.
If a fracture occurs in bone and it is small, you shall extract it with care.
And you shall tell the cure for skull fractures that are poisonous.
On neck wounds.
If a wound occurs on the neck, there is no difference in treatment compared to a simple skull fracture.
If the nerve or artery in the neck is cut or severed, it is not advisable to treat with anything that causes violent constriction, such as zinjari and zegi, which are poisonous to nerves and harmful to them. And do not place any pressure on the wound at all, because the substance of nerves is sensitive and their continuation is near to the brain, which is above all other members.
If the wound is cured with light medicines, such as cleaning it with sweet water, sometimes infused in rose oil or not-rose oil, and sometimes cleaned with sweet water and other remedies according to this method, and then anointed with moisture.
But if the wound is large, administer suture, or sew the lips (of the wound), and apply care with powders within it until it heals.
But if the wound is deep or occurs in the lower part of the skull, you shall perforate it in the most depressed area.
But if an artery is cut in the wound and there is bleeding, you shall separate or stop the flow of blood by ligating it, or cauterize it if necessity requires it.
But if the spinal vertebrae of the neck are cut and the veins of the neck are saved, you shall sew the edges of the wound on the back of the neck over the spine of the neck and do not touch it; elevate it equally and align it with its natural shape, and strengthen it with a decent structure, and allow it to rest for several days; apply care with liniments where there is stiffness and excitation until it heals.
If the wound has entered the bone and there is danger of fragmentation of the bones, you shall percuss it (tap gently with a hammer or similar instrument), and if there are pieces of bone that can be extracted, extract all of them; for what cannot be handled, allow it to rest until the wound becomes more prominent, and separate it from the surrounding tissue and its edge should be relieved.
And if the wound is recent, you shall sprinkle powder over it that has been recommended to you. But if the wound is made into a fistula (abnormal passage), you shall treat it with that which the memory brings to mind in its own chapter.
If it is a spear or sword wound, and sometimes with great depth, you shall consider more if the patient has shortness of breath when he speaks; you shall know that the wound is fatal.
And if the wound is not deep and the bleeding is recent from the patient's own blood, you shall pause at the first hour with powder, and not apply pressure to it so that the blood does not remain trapped in the depth of the wound and return to the heart and weaken the patient, but rather wait for an attractive liniment, and remove it every two days.
And if the liniment is not available, you shall pause at the orifice of the wound and allow any pus that is there to drain out; make sure the patient sleeps on the wound so that what is in place runs out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound occurred and the patient does not suffer from spasm, tremor of heart bad nor chest tightness, sometimes with other good dispositions of it, you shall know that the wound is healed; then treat it with ointments and other care until it heals.
But if the healing of it is excused and it remains open for a long time, you shall know that it has become a fistula (abnormal passage); treat it accordingly in its own chapter.
And if the wound is from a spear or sword and it is simple on the surface of the chest or back, you shall treat it with suture if it is large, or powder if it is small.
But if danger has already occurred in the bone and it is only cut pieces of flesh, you shall twist the wound and try to extract those pieces of flesh if they are separated. If they are not separated, you shall allow them to rest until the wound becomes more prominent, as extracting them is relieved.
And for other accidents that occur in other members, the help in care is the same as what has been said.
Of the afflictions of the belly.
The indisposition that happens in the abdomen is either large, small, or medium-sized.
A small disruption is treated with difficulty by other means, and it is known that its reduction is more difficult unless you take care to insert it into its place immediately, for it swells up and thickens, making the insertion of it difficult.
And therefore the best disruption is medium-sized, for in that case, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in both cases mentioned above.
And know that the suture of the abdomen is done in four ways, but two of these are common to those sutures of other afflictions of the body.
Two other particular sutures communicate with other afflictions, but they are more specific to the suture of the abdomen.
And I recommend that you have extensive exposure and detailed explanation.
Therefore say that when the affliction is small and there is some cause of the intestine by it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then one of two methods can be used: either because of the smallness of the disruption as we have said, or when the intestine has been inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when it is so, it is necessary that you warm the intestine in such a way that you immerse a sponge or damp cloth in hot water only, or you take [the intestine] in hot water and cyperum, spice, and bind it with it until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes you will find one in which there is stricture, and it is better that water alone be used to resolve the inflammation.
And therefore, when the inflammation is resolved, you moisturize the intestine with water in which salt and wormwood are contained, for this eases the insertion of it with little effort.
But if the insertion is blocked by this treatment, then you cut the disruption slightly with an instrument that is used to incise hemorrhoids.
And the primary treatment is better when it is not cutting, and there should be no concern for cutting unless necessary and unavoidable.
Of the instrument, consider its form:
One side of the instrument is curved and sharp, while the other side is not curved or pointed, and its tip should be slightly blunt, not as sharp as a spatula's.
And therefore when the affliction increases and the intestine comes out, its reduction should be according to its natural form in its proper place if possible, for it is better.
But when the disruption is large and located in the lower part of the abdomen, you should make the patient lie on their back and elevate their legs as much as possible; if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should elevate their head and feet as much as possible.
And similarly, if the disruption is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, you should pause your thoughts and consideration immediately so that the side on which the affliction is located is elevated more than the other.
And therefore you must attend to this in large and medium-sized afflictions.
In small afflictions, pause what is ready for use and make sure that the assistant who will administer it has your full trust, as he will hold the disruption with his entire hand and adjust the lips of it, and approach the incision from one side to avoid causing any other damage.
This is the common method of suturing: one that takes a needle or counting of needles according to the size of the affliction; and approach it from the end of the disruption of the ring finger, tighten the needle once, without inserting it into both ends of the thread, with both ends of the ciphax that is inside the parts, until you make it penetrate through that part; and you have already added two ends of the thread and two ends of the ciphax, and they are made in four rows.
And tighten with double thread around the needle, and sometimes from two sides at once, and gather both lips and the affliction by proper approximation; and approach the thickness of the finger and insert another needle in it, and approach it as you did with the first needle; and do not stop doing this if it is necessary with the needles until you have completed the contraction of the affliction.
And needles can be medium-sized between coarseness and fineness, as fine needles penetrate the flesh lightly; while thick ones are difficult to insert into the thread, and therefore it is necessary that they be medium in both coarseness and fineness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be inserted at two opposite ends of the wound, so that the flesh is not cut lightly and the ulcer heals before its consolidation.
And it is not necessary to extend the stitching, so that the wound is not prevented from healing.
Then sew the ends of the wounds together so that you do not injure the patient while he sleeps; and place pulverized plaster all around the stitching, which will have the ends of the needles; and let it remain until you know that the wound has healed.
And these types of stitching with needles in this manner are suitable for small wounds, because the stitching of them requires only one or two needles around that area.

Treatment for abscesses and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess that becomes old and is not healed and remains wet all the time and does not drain off, whether it is in any part of the body, is universally called a fistula and we call it an abscess.
A fistula, according to truth, is a hard, white nodule, without pain and with a concavity similar to the concavity of an ulcer; and it is therefore called "rizzated abscess", i.e. ulcer.
And in some men it is a wet fistula that drains pus; and its moisture is discharged in some hours. And sometimes this moisture moves, and it is small, thick, and thin.
I have shown all these causes, their symptoms and the treatment of them in the division.
Therefore, it is necessary for you to know the treatment of fistulas, which is performed by operation with a surgeon.
And know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; for they are fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the bladder, or the hernia, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the spine, or one of the joints composed in such a way as the hand and the foot; and they are those that provide proximity to the main member and similar ones.
And thus that fistula which follows this method belongs to difficult-to-heal ailments and does not receive cure, and care for it is labor and ignorance.
If the depth of one of these fistulas does not reach these places as stated, and you hope for its healing and believe in it, and wish to understand more about it, then take a probe or iron, and if the fistula follows a straight path, touch it with it.

Chapter on fracture when it occurs in the head.
The types of fractures are multiple, and the shapes of them are various, and the causes are distinct, for some fracture is caused by a blow with a sharp object that penetrates the whole bone, which is then broken, as happens in the leg, and this type of fracture is called sciatic; or the sharp object cuts into one part of the bone and cuts through the flesh but does not penetrate to the other side; and this type of fracture is called absolute levation.
The wound from these two types of fractures can be large or small, and the fracture can be concave or convex; the cause of it is striking with heavy objects or falling on stones, and so forth.
This fracture can either penetrate the subcutaneous tissue (pannicol), which is located just below the bone, or it can occur on the surface of the bone, and the wound from these two types of fractures can be large or small.
And of the fracture that is reduced in the bone, in the subtlety of the hair follicle, small, and for this reason, this type of fracture is called capillary fracture.
The fracture that is caused by falling or striking with stones, and similar occurrences, causes one layer of the bone to separate from its edges, and it occurs in the concave area, such as when it happens to the vertebrae when they are struck and separated at their edges.
Moreover, this is done in bones that are soft, such as in the spines of children.
And all these types of fractures have similar symptoms, such as separated bones. And you will come with remedy for all in their place.
I will discern all kinds of fracture through examination and by testing for them and the removal of corrupt flesh that is over them.
A capillary fracture is recognized by being uncovered on the bone, and it is scraped off, and it turns white when tested, for a fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fracture, it is first necessary to consider the conditions of the patient, and afterwards if you see signs that indicate fear, such as vomiting of bile, extension, loss of consciousness, abscesses in the throat, fainting, acute fever, inflammation or redness of the eyes, and similar signs in the greater dispositions, one doubts that it is healed.
And if there are no unusual signs, you hope for health, then you begin the treatment of it.
The cause is that if it has come to you in the beginning like a fracture and that is during winter days, then you must remove the bone splint no later than the twelfth day according to all dispositions.
If it is during summer days, then you must remove the bone splint no later than the seventh day, because if the bone remains untreated for too long, it may rot due to the fat, and the signs that I mentioned before may occur.
And if the fracture has reached the fat covering the brain, and it is fragmented into small parts and sticky, then you must pack the fractured area with compressed material according to my recommendation.
And you raise the head of the patient with fracture, and uncover the bone according to your custom and as possible according to the shape of the injury, and it should be comfortable for the patient.
And if it occurs to you, you must examine the bone, search for bleeding or an abscess, and then oppose yourself with what is necessary, which is to place damp cloths soaked in wine and olive oil over the injury, and allow the wound to rest until the abscess is relieved, and make sure that the bleeding has stopped; and begin with the circumcision of the bone and excision of it.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
The first method is that you insert the bone splint with a narrow incision tool of this kind: And place another incision tool of slightly greater size on it; and this is its shape:
Place the wider incision tool on top of the narrow one.
And it is necessary for you to have a number of incision tools of different sizes, some larger than others, and some shorter than the others and ending in sharp points, and made of iron or steel.
Use them with ease when striking the incision tool to avoid hitting the head and damaging it.
If the bone is hard and strong, then you must perforate around it before applying the incision tools with levers, which are called non-penetrating levers.
They are not called that because the lever does not reach the end of the bone, so at the lever's tip is rounded or edge on top of the sharp head of it, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the bone.
It is appropriate for you to take a large number of these levers, the quantity of which should be according to the thickness of each bone, until it reaches the end of every part of the skull, the sharpness of which may vary in length and width according to the thickness of the bone.
This is the shape of three types of levers: The purpose of perforating around the broken bone is to place the lever on the bone and rotate it with your hands until you feel that the bone has been penetrated; and then move the lever to another location, and place it where the amount of thickness of the rod or environment is maximum.
And insert the incision tools between each crack in the bone and use the smallest one that you can on it until you have cut through the bone or with your hand or some other means using various tools such as tweezers and pliers, which you will prepare for this purpose.
And it is necessary that you take care that the incision tool does not touch any nerve.
Therefore, when you perforate the bone, if it is continuous with the nerve, you should separate it from the nerve and extract it, and then cut and file away the sharp edges of the bone with another tool similar to an incision tool but much thinner.
And if any small fragments or splinters of bones remain, you should take care of them with the tools that are necessary for you; and then treat the wound with linen and ointment as I have said before.
The other method is light and free of fear, which is known as Guillaume's operation and is highly praised; and this is its explanation: It is necessary for you at the beginning to begin discovering the fracture of the bone in the place where it is most obvious and visible until when you have found that fragment and stopped at its end, the tip of the following incision tool should be placed gently and slowly over it. The shape of the incision tool is as follows:
The first sharp incision is made along two sides advancing in length, as sometimes occurs; the second rounded incision should be adjusted at the hipbone with the edge of the first sharp incision in the bone.
Then strike with the blunt side of the incision tool into a small depression that cuts through all the bone with ease as it rotates, and you must be assured that no panic will occur in the event that you fear something might happen in any way; if the healer was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them, certainly, he would be asleep.
If any cause of panic remains attached to the bone of the incision, you will make an incision with the rounded edge of the incision tool in that location of the bone, and it will be delivered easily, since it is delivered from there without harm or fear.
If the bone fracture does not penetrate into the incision, and what is abscessed or remains from the bone is its surface, and it retains roughness and subtle fissures, then you must smooth that roughness and remove those fissures with fine razors that you have prepared in various quantities so that it is possible for you to administer them in all places the most suitable and the most correct according to where your operation goes wrong with the broken bone and the shape of it.
You must administer the razor with which you smooth the bone at first from the side of those razors, and then use it near the subtle part of it, and do not cease to do so continuously until you reach administering the pus and all the subtle parts of them.
In each of the other small and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to what you lost during correction.
It is because not all those who have studied in that art are equally skilled. And we write about what they have demonstrated in large fractures.
If the incision is rectangular when you smooth the bone, then you must take linen cloth according to the size of the wound and immerse it in warm oil, and place it over the wound; and take another linen cloth folded or tripled and immerse it in vinegar and oil, and place it on top of the first cloth and perform a light massage on the pus that you can without injuring the bone; and apply ointment to it, but do not press it too much, only as much as the wound requires.
Leave that with this care for a day or two until you are sure of cauterization; then loosen it and administer medicines with which excessive dryness is superfluous, such as linseed oil, flour of rye, powder of asafoetida and aristolochia, and similar to these.
You should make powder of these medicines that is pulverized over the wound, such as drying ones; and in general, administer all medicine of the property of which is that it absorbs but does not burn.
Be careful that the wound in your care is clean; do not let it get infected with any filth or anything else; nor leave virulence there; because when it is placed on the skull of the brain, it corrupts it and poisons it, causing harm to the patient with a wicked malignant illness.
If it happens on the skull of the head, when the bone is corrected and treatment is applied to it, and becomes darker in its surface; then consider if the accidents that we have said happen to the patient, you will know that they are harmful without any doubt.
If it does not get darker due to the medicine that is applied on it, but rather due to the nature of the medicine, then you must take honey and warm oil in three parts. And with that linen cloth, place it on the skull; and care for it with the spices of healing until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when broken.
Know that the nose is not broken into two parts together, but rather one of those parts is elevated above the other, because there are two bones in it; and the fracture does not occur in one of them, but rather it bends, twists, and swells.
If the bone is broken in one of its two divisions, then it is necessary that you insert your little finger into the nose and guide the fracture within with your other finger, and apply pressure from outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. Do this for ease, but be careful not to harm the patient due to your treatment.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger does not reach it, then you must align the extremity of the ray where there is a small swelling.
If the fracture is in two places, then you should make an approximation (of what was done).
Cure the patient in the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then return the wound to rest for seven or ten days under a cauterized dressing.
Insert a piece of cloth into the nose if the fracture is in one place, or two small ones (cotton balls), if the fracture is in two places, and make them as thick as necessary to fill the hollow of the nose.
As the old restorers say, the cotton balls should be sewn in felt and kept for a day, but I do not see it; however, the cloth must be soaked in hog's fat with powdered millstone, and then leave it until the bone is set and the cartilage hardened.
Enter the nose every few days instead of cotton balls, since there is too much pus that is wrapped in a lion's cloth; and if the fracture of the nose is very painful and the patient is not forbidden from breathing.
This is not necessary for you; if you wish, do it, and if you wish, use cotton balls.
And if another seven days have passed and the wound is cauterized, fill the nose with wax or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or with honey.
But if the wound is not cauterized, then fill it from outside with sizzling flour and powdered ashes mixed with albumen of eggs, and place a cloth over it (to prevent it) and do not touch the nose for any reason.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small or comminuted fracture, then you must cut them, extract them using the instruments appropriate for the case; and sew up the incision, cure it with what heals and consolidates, and apply ointments as necessary.
If there is an ulcer inside the nose, you must cure it with cotton and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jawbone when it is broken.
When the jawbone breaks and the fracture is not an ulcer, observe whether the fracture is only on the outside, and it is not broken in two and curved towards the inside. If this is the case, its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left index finger in the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jawbone, insert your right index finger into the mouth; and press with one hand the swelling of the fracture towards the inside with ease at the outer parts, and with the other hand at the outer part of the bone; do this gently.
And if the jawbone fracture is already separated into two parts, you must administer extension to both sides according to right angles until it is possible for them to be aligned.
If there is a dislocation of teeth due to shock, you must stabilize those that you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or suture; and place cotton over the broken jawbone, place cotton over the double cloth, and place a large flat stone gently made, or a single piece of felt long enough for the jawbone; and hold it in place according to what is suitable for the injury and the application, until it is not destroyed.
Order the patient to rest and peace, and prevent the sour-tasting lemons from eating.
If you suspect that the shape has been altered in some way, you must examine it on the third day and correct what has already been altered; then fill it with powdered millstone, albumen of eggs or flour of sesame, since you will have removed the wax, and place a woolen cloth over the ointment.
And thus, when the ointment is attached and adjusted to the bone, and its position is not altered, leave it alone, as it will not be removed until it heals and strengthens the fracture; because many weeks have passed since this fracture was tightened in three weeks.
And if another seven days have passed and the wound is cauterized, administer what was said earlier for its treatment until it heals, and remove the cautery.
And if the fracture is an ulcer, consider whether it is a separated piece of bone or not, you must carefully remove those pieces with the tools appropriate for the case.
And if the orifice of the ulcer is narrow, you must widen it with a curette according to your need.
When you have stopped the bleeding from the wound, and it does not continue to flow, then sew up the opening of the sore if it is wide; or apply a dressing that suits the case until it heals.
If the swelling in the body part continues to grow, even after the application of leeches, and the wound does not heal within ten days, then make an incision into it with a knife, and apply a poultice or plaster that suits the case, until it heals.
On the repair of the collarbone when it is broken.
A broken collarbone that is fractured in front must be treated by cutting the muscles on the side of the break and setting the bone fragments in one of three ways.
If the fracture is simple, comminuted, or compound (with an open wound); and if it does not cause severe bleeding or swelling; then it is easier to treat. However, if the fracture causes severe bleeding or swelling, it is difficult to treat, or the fracture is accompanied by a sore.
And the procedure for treating a fracture that is accompanied by a sore, is to have two assistants present, one of whom will hold the broken collarbone and guide it back into place, while the other extends the neck on the side of the break.
Then align the fracture with the teeth, until it is set in its proper form, and not too deep or concave.
If more extension is needed, then place a cloth or linen under the patient's armpit and make it as large as your need requires. Then extend and elevate the arm, and support it with your hand until you align the fracture in its proper position.
If you are unable to extend the arm beyond the shoulder joint due to swelling or pain, then have the patient lie on their back, and place a soft cushion under one elbow (depending on the size needed).
Then the assistant pulls on the muscle of the arm towards the elbow until the bones in the collarbone are elevated above the shoulder joint, and then you correct the fracture and align it with the teeth.
But if the collarbone is painful and swollen, then you must make an incision over it and extract the pus with ease.
And if it is lodged in the bone, you must make an incision over it with one of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, since you have placed the chisel under the collarbone. The chisel has a handle or a shaft of wood or iron and its shape is as follows:
It is shaped like a mortar or pestle with a concave bottom, and its width should be as needed for the bone or fracture, depending on its size or depth.
But the length of the chisel should be as long as is practical for the operation, with two ends so that one end is broad and the other narrow.
If the wound caused by the incision that you have made to extract pus from the bone is wide, and it appears that there is no more pus coming out, then you must sew the edges of the wound together with stitches.
If the disruption is small or you fear an abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
If an abscess occurs, then you should insert cloth soaked in warm vinegar and oil of roses and wait.
And it is important that the splint not be too tight over the broken bone; therefore, you must observe the patient every day to ensure that the swelling is reduced or the splint has been removed, at which point you should correct and strengthen the immobilization.
Place the patient's head on the pillow and support their neck with a small cushion to ensure that they sleep slightly raised at night; in this way, the assistance provided to the latissimus dorsi muscle is elevated, causing the fracture of the clavicle to heal by raising it. Or, place the patient's arms along their chest.
Do not remove the immobilization unless an infection or abscess occurs at the site within twelve days.
Reapply the plaster if you see any signs of infection at the site, adjust it to fit the immobilization, and leave it in place until the clavicle fracture is healed and has formed a callus.
The immobilization should be strengthened and supported for about 28 days, and in some cases, it may take longer, even several months for the healing process to occur.
Chapter VI.
On the treatment of a shoulder fracture.
The scapula is broken in its lateral part and not at the tip but along its edges.
Since the scapula is broken in its lateral part and not at the tip, but rather along its edges, when it is fractured in this manner and cannot be seen without touching, you should prepare for surgery and reduce the fracture according to its natural shape using any engineering method that is possible.
Place powder made of ground wheat flour mixed with egg white and lion's stopper on the fracture site, apply double layers of cloth, place a thin wooden stick across the shoulder blade according to the size of the scapula or slightly more.
But if the wooden stick is concave in any of the scapula's locations, you should fill that concavity with lion's stopper, and adjust the stick accordingly.
Strengthen it above with warm steam and hold the stick firmly in place until it no longer protrudes from its original position.
Consider the immobilization every day, and whenever it becomes softened, replace it with a new one and adjust and straighten the stick if it protrudes from its original position.
Keep the patient in a comfortable position.
The scapula heals within 20 days or up to 35 days.
Once this period has elapsed, you should remove the immobilization, and be sure that the bones from which it began have not been affected or their dissolution has not occurred.
If the bone protrudes from the socket and you place a ligament over it, then you should cut through the ligament, remove the bone, and follow the instructions given for treating a clavicle fracture if an abscess occurs in this case.
Chapter VII.
On the treatment of an elbow fracture.
The elbow is sometimes broken in its middle part and extends into the joint, but more often, the ends of the bone are fractured and compressed.
And when the bone is broken among its parts, it tends towards fracture and occurs great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, and heavy lethargy and vomiting; and it is apparent in the cavity of the broken bone and not hidden from sensation.
The restoration is that the patient should be placed on their back, and a cushion placed between their shoulders; and press upon the muscles of it, and adjust its ribs with the edge of two fingers, subtly perform the operation and in accordance with all possible means and what is appropriate, until the figure of the bone returns as it was.
Then cover and pack it with plaster and gauze and rest upon a thin board of willow or alder and similarly in lightness, because you wrap it in cloth; and subtly tie it over the broken bone so that it is not crooked; and go along its length according to the roundness of the back some times, and tighten it properly; and consider the length at all times, and when it becomes soft, tighten it again.
And if it is necessary to remove the plaster or pus that occurs in the area, you must corrode or cauterize it, or pain, or abscess, then cut and loosen it and remove the plaster and correct what has occurred by means of treatment; and reduce the plaster, sometimes changing its face; and adjust it with proper tightening until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of broken ribs.
Know that a fracture of ribs does not occur in thin places, but only where they are located near the chest; and at the extremities of its division from the front, it does not contract because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of this is not hidden from sensation, you must examine it with palpation.
And the restoration should be such that you guide the fracture with your fingers according to the possible method, until it is aligned as it should be, and cover and tighten the broken bone with a splint if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs is tending towards the division inside, it causes strong pain and puncture in the patient, as in puncture that occurs in those who have pleurisy, and for this reason you cover the bones with a gauze; and it causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancient physicians were ingenious in treating this with various means.
And they said: it is necessary to stop the food of the patient that generates swelling and flatulence, so that the abdomen is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed towards the division outside.
But we avoid that for fear it may cause abscesses, unless they occur; and if they do occur, then help with that.
And some of them said that it should be avoided wind and that it should drain with force; and this is reasoning, but it is feared that the wind attracts superfluidity to the area due to weakness.
And some of them said: it should be covered with wool that is already soaked in hot oil, and powder applied between the ribs until it is filled, so that the length is aligned when you wrap it according to roundness; and take care of the patient with treatment for pleurisy and medicine.
And if the strong constriction of the patient who cannot bear it, and the bones are covered with a gauze with puncture, fear is felt for the patient, then it is necessary to cut through the area, uncover the broken rib, and cover it with an instrument that preserves the fracture, the memory of which was mentioned earlier, and cut through the bone with ease, and extract it.
And that we align the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with bandages until it heals.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then cut and pour powder in rose oil and cover the area, and treat the patient according to what is necessary for an internal abscess.
And lie on the bed until healed.
Chapter IX.
Chapter X.
Sometimes the fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and then they tighten, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back. And if you want to know whether it will heal or not, consider whether the muscles are already softened and stupefied and dead, and cannot strengthen or extend them, and when you press on them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle without feeling pain in them, then you know that it is not healed, and in these cases it is mortal.
And if the muscles are swollen and there is constriction and piercing with fingers, then you know that the neck of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
If you see the vertebrae of the neck resembling such [condition] and wish to know if it will heal or not, then consider its feet.
Here is the translation: If it becomes soft and swollen like that which happens in other places and when it lies on its back, it shows signs of inflammation and excess fluid retention without its will, and sometimes it rolls over onto its back and wishes to urinate but cannot, then you know that it is fatal, and at that point, do not bother with the treatment of it.
If no such cause occurs, the situation is better and easier to deal with.
The treatment is to apply cauterization to the abscess that has formed on the swollen vertebra, or with oily soaked mullein leaves; place it over that area for several days until the abscess is cauterized.
Then place a comforting and soothing plaster on the affected area, apply strong medicine to it; and command the patient to rest, remain calm, and not sleep on the affected side until it is healed.
If there is need for the removal of the affected bone or any other cause that has been separated from it, then cut the skin and expose the bone; align the edges of the wound, and stitch if necessary; and care for it with a healing ointment until it is healed.
But if the lower end of the bone in the heel is broken and the skin is torn off, then you must insert your left hand into the anus and pull out the broken bone with the right hand according to what is possible by equalizing it; and apply a plaster and splint if necessary, and strengthen it.
And if you feel fractures in it [the bone], then split it open, remove the fracture, and care for the wound with the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the repair of the ankle.
The bones of the ankles are broken and if they break, their fracture is not anything other than communication between the ends and they are inserted along the length and decline into the sides; pain and puncture occur at the site and the leg is paralyzed when it is fractured.
The repair of it [the ankle] is to stretch your hand over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at the end of it [the bone], then you pull it out as much as is appropriate according to what is necessary for alignment with its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the long bone or descends to its sides, then you make the patient lie on their stomach until the repair of that fracture is prepared. And once you have pulled it out [the bone], place a plaster over it and place an immobilizer of wood or skin over that, and tighten it with bandaging without fear of re-fracture or removal of the splint.
Pull the concavity with whatever fills it until it takes on the alignment, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their unaffected side.
But if an abscess occurs, then you cease the extension and repair of it until the abscess is cauterized, and place something over it as prescribed; and return to the repair of it, and strengthen it in that manner.
And if there are swollen vertebrae or they are shattered, then you treat them.
Chapter 11.
On the repair of the humerus fracture.
The humerus is that which is between the shoulder and the head of the arm.
If it is broken, the repair of it [the humerus] is done in one of two ways, of which one is to take a wooden pole of medium thickness as follows:
The tendons are in two ends and should be suspended from a high place.
Carry the patient on a stretcher; then throw out his broken arm beyond the post until it reaches the tiptop in the place where the curvature of the post is attached.
Suspend the patient with some serious cause, or let the servant extend it to the sides; then call for the doctor who is familiar with fractures and keep him with it until the fracture returns as it should.
The other method is that the patient lies down on his back and supports his neck with one hand; then command two servants, one to hold whatever is above the fracture with his hands, and the other to hold the foot below; each of them should extend to their side.
If you want the extension to be stronger, then press hard on the fracture with both hands and have each of the servants extend to two sides.
Therefore, if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary to place a cushion under the middle of the limb and the other on the side of the fracture to keep it compressed.
Similarly, if the fracture is at the joint, then it is necessary to place the limb on that location and compress the joint as well.
Hold it with ease and without trouble, and apply pressure when it is necessary and correctly placed in a good manner; then press it if no pus comes out (abscess).
But if an abscess occurs, leave the constriction for seven days and apply wool soaked in vinegar and olive oil to it until the abscess subsides; then press on it.
The method of pressing on the fracture is to place a plaster on it, then cover it with a linen bandage, and adjust the arm using a splint; place your hand open over the muscle.
Place the cloth and press on the splint to hold the arm in place so that it is in the correct position, unless there is some reason due to a broken bone.
Use splints (stents).
Place the wider and stronger splint over the fracture, placing the width of the finger between each splint and making the length of the splints according to the breakage with an addition of three fingers on all sides.
Then press on the splints as described at the beginning of the chapter.
Your constriction over the location of the fracture should be tighter. The longer the break, the looser the compression.
Therefore, if you see that the position of the splints and compression is as described in the time when you restore the limb to its natural position, then do it.
And if you fear an abscess, leave the constriction (or bandaging) and let the probes (or needles) stay in for up to seven days as we have said.
Then consider the linen in all parts for three days to prevent inflammation or pressure in the area; or food is prohibited from reaching the member due to the excess constriction of the bandaging; and therefore, correct everything as we have said.
If you are in doubt about any cause of this (the condition), then do not undo the bandaging except after three days or more.
Let the patient lie on their back, and let the hands be on their stomach.
Place a hot poultice filled with wool on the broken member, and consider during the night or day that neither the member's figure nor the bandaging should be undone or removed; or the linen should be softened, therefore correct everything with your judgment.
The food of the patient should be as previously ordered, only the food should be subtle at first until it begins to strengthen; and then you must make the food coarser for him.
The hot poultice and the blanket of the patient are usually strengthened for 40 days; and then they should be undone, and a bath and medicine should be administered which will correct it (the condition).
If the fracture is fresh, then leave the bandaging and probes in for days or up to two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
An arm is composed of two bones that are called the ulna and radius; the smaller one is located near the elbow, and the larger one is below it (the lower part of the arm).
If only the larger bone is broken, or if both bones are fractured together, it is a severe break.
If only the smaller bone near the elbow is broken, then the restoration of it is easy and healing is quick.
And if the bone below the break is broken as well, then the injury is bad and healing is difficult.
It is even worse when both bones are fractured together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller one near the elbow, it is appropriate for the doctor to carry out the restoration of it with a small and shallow incision, with ease until the wound is closed.
But if the larger bone is the one that is fractured, then you must make a deep and powerful incision.
And if both bones are fractured together, then you must make the extraction (removal of the broken bone fragments) less forceful.
You must make the hand's figure with the restoration of it and the extended incision over a long incision; and the palm of the hand is at the upper parts, as previously said, and the ear lobe is lower than the others mentioned, and the patient lies on a low couch.
Place a small incision in front of it (the wound) at the height or level of it, so that the patient does not have to work excessively.
The assistant should extend the arm above from the wrist with his hand or bandage, and another assistant should do the same on top of it (the broken bone).
The doctor should wrap the bone in a cloth to reduce it as best as possible.
If there are fragments of bone in the fracture, then you should strive to remove all the pieces at the site with your judgment.
If a fragment of bone appears separated that punctures the skin and is not hopeful for restoration, then you should make an incision on it and remove it using the method previously mentioned.
If the fracture is open, then refer to the previous chapter for its treatment.
If an abscess appears at the beginning of your restoration, then line a cloth with a wax made from rose oil and white beeswax, and it should be in between thickness and delicacy, and strengthen the cloth over it with a delicate bandage until the abscess subsides; and then remove the wax and place an embrocation prepared with powder of milled charcoal and clay of huou, and let probes be placed; and the probe that is placed over the fracture should be sharp and strong.
Know that the name of the stars in the arm is given in major arrangements, or it is a fracture in one joint or in two joints at once.
Then pause your splint at the site of the fracture firmly and strongly, and when the swelling is more at the parts above the fracture or at the lower parts of it, pause the splint a little less tightly according to whether the reminder of it begins at the start of the chapter.
The cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp, not hard or rough, and the person who is to bind it should be moderately skilled between being subtle and thick, as we have recommended.
Consider the member and the limb for several days. And if any cause arises that requires rectification, such as the swelling occurring in the member, then you must bind the member with cold water until the swelling ceases.
Leave the member unbound until it stops swelling and then return the splint.
If the swelling has subsided and the bone has been reabsorbed and seems similar to these, then you must correct everything with your skill.
Consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from reaching the member due to excess swelling, as then you must reduce that slightly and leave it for several days until the nourishment returns, and strengthen it.
If the patient does not suffer from any of the aforementioned causes, you do not need to loosen it for approximately twenty days or thereabouts.
Place the patient's hand in suspension due to its ability to reduce agitated movements, and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or forty days, or weakly it is restored in twenty-eight days, and all of this depends on the complications and the disposition of the skill.
Chapter 13
On the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and fingers.
Know that the thumb and the joints of fingers often suffer fractures, but do not treat them unless they are severely displaced.
When the hand is fractured or severely displaced, you must make the patient lie down with his arm elevated according to the level of injury, and then pause the hand over it in an extended position.
The minister should extend the broken bones and the surgeon should align them together until they are properly aligned.
You must apply plaster and gauze, unless there is an abscess caution; then pause a steel rod according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If the fracture is inward within the hollow parts, you should press the abdomen with the palm of your hand, and then make a round shape like a piece of cloth, and command the patient to squeeze it with their broken palm, keep it tight with long fabric.
The needle of the heart should be with cloth that is lightweight so that the heart is applied to the palm, and squeeze it firmly as needed.
If the fracture is outside the hollow parts, then press down on both sides of the fracture with your palms, so that the hands are open and straight; hold it firmly as needed, so that it is turned over like a handle between the two with linen bandage.
If there is a fracture in one of the knuckles (of the fingers), if it is open, then close it as needed and hold it with the palm and if desired, place a small straight splint on it to lift the fracture and prevent it from becoming worse.
If the fracture is in other fingers (such as the middle finger, index finger, ring finger or little finger), then close it and tie it with a linen strip where the finger is, or all of them together if possible, which is better; or place a small splint on it as previously mentioned for the knuckle.
And consider in your treatment and apply the cauterized abscess and opposite position - with the operation - to what is needed when there is any cause of such a thing, as the narration has been repeated about it.
Chapter XIV.
On the restoration of the bone fracture of the thigh.
The bones of the thigh, when moved, are broken and declared by examination according to whether it turns to the front or swollen part.
The restoration is done in such a way that the bandage is tightened over the fracture and another bandage on the opposite side of the fracture, and the patient lies on their stomach and on their face.
And extend all the bandages to the appropriate side according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If it is proximate to the hip of the thigh, then extend the bandage towards the lower side at the front of the thigh, so that the extension contains it, and another bandage on the opposite side of the fracture.
Similarly, if the fracture is proximate to the lower part, then make the bandage towards the lower part and let the extension be at the front parts.
The doctor should close the bone with both hands inside to reduce it as much as possible in its natural position and the bone is joined by a good connection; then it is necessary to place plaster and bandage, unless there is an abscess on the member. If there is an abscess, you should leave it for some days until the cauterized abscess is healed, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be done in this way: cover the fracture with a hard bandage, fold it over two or three times and remove any excess; then wrap the leg towards the lower part and towards the hip of the thigh.
Insert a long needle between the thigh and the lower leg near the front part, and let the ends of the needle come out from the swollen part on both sides, and turn it over the thigh and leg as much as possible with the excess bandage.

Extend the needle to both ends of the leg towards the lower part up to the ankle of the foot, and hold both ends of the other splints on the opposite side so that the bandage does not slip from its place.
Place the bandage over this so that it does not cause corruption, cauterized abscess or inflammation and resemble those; and if there is any such condition, then consult with the doctor immediately, and correct all of it as needed according to what has been previously said.
If there is a tumor in the bone, you should close it as much as possible; if not, let it fester and remove it, and heal it with what has been previously mentioned until it is healed.

Dislocation discussion (or Treatise on Dislocations)
A dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of the body, which prevents movement, and distorts the figure of the limb, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to act promptly to reduce it, and not to delay its reduction, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes swollen and soft tissue damage is done, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason, it is not good to be sick or lying down during the swelling period, as many times it brings spasms and painful cramps.
But when this happens, it is necessary that you attend to the patient's flabotomy (incision), and prepare immediately to drain the swelling; and wrap the member in warm water and oil, and reduce it with ease.
And all members should be cured with the medicine that will bring back their original position (or healing).
And you have already arranged the chapters of dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture: from the upper part of the body down to the lower.
Chapter XXIV.
Treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If both jaws are dislocated, they should not be left like that; and a dislocation of either jaw can occur in two ways: one is reduced gently by moving them slightly back into their original position, or it is completely dislocated and needs to be mollified at the juncture before saliva from the patient's mouth flows out and they cannot close their lips, and their tongue becomes wrapped in its fold.
But if the dislocation is slight, it can be reduced with minimal effort.
But if the dislocation is complete and extensive, it requires gentle and prompt reduction.
The attendant should hold the patient's head, and the doctor should insert their thumb into the appropriate place inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is in one part; or they should insert both thumbs simultaneously, if the dislocation is in both parts, and the other attendants should be outside with them to support. The doctor should then instruct the patient to stop moving their jaws and allow them to return to their original position; and the doctor should hold the jaw and wrap it until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, administer warm water and oil to facilitate easy reduction and do not delay the reduction of both. As previously mentioned.
And once they have been reduced and wrapped, the patient's mouth should be closed and not allowed to move, and you can apply powdered fabric with wax (beeswax) coated in oil to the area and let it soften gently.
The patient should sleep on their back, and their head should be placed between two pillows to prevent it from leaning to the right or left; do not force them to swallow anything; let their food be lean meat until the pain has subsided and the jaws have healed.
And this should be done with ease, and do not strain the opening of the mouth during eating, drinking, speaking, or chewing, until the jaw has healed.
If the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their original position, this may lead to recurring fevers and constant sweating, and a strong urge for vomiting may occur, accompanied by pure blood.
And when you see this, you should know that it is a bad sign; and many times the patient dies within ten days.

Chapter 27.
On the treatment of shoulder dislocation.

Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of shoulder, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.

Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of dislocation of fingers.
Fingers are sometimes dislocated in various places.
When a finger of these is dislocated at the palm or belly of it, you should squeeze the finger and press the dislocation with your thumb until it returns.
Approach should be to support the head of the finger, and suspend the part where it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Approach should be to extend it (the dislocated finger) and suspend it until it is straightened on that same day.
When night comes, you should follow this procedure, and do not cease to loosen it during the day and exercise movement, and leave it at night; do so for several days until it is strengthened.
Do the same thing if it is dislocated in the belly of the hand, and leave it to that location and perform the first operation as described above, until it is healed.
Do the same thing when it is dislocated in other locations.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of dislocation of spondylus (vertebra) of the back (spine).
When one of the vertebrae in the back or neck experiences complete dislocation and remains displaced from its place, there is no cure for it as death approaches the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's bowel movement is out of their control and they cannot retain it; many times various body parts or legs or arms, or one of them, are weakened, or a vertebra has been displaced from its place, because weakness means that a vertebra has been dislocated and it is removed in four pieces.
Therefore, it has been removed in pieces at the back and is called gibbosity.
But the cure for it is to consider whether gibbosity occurs in childhood, because in that case there is neither a cure nor healing of the entire body.
But in cases caused by accidents or blows or similar to these, the Ancients described the methods of cure at length, but I have abridged it from that source for a shorter explanation, since it is closer to the clearer exposition, which I have expanded and arranged using various instruments, with which I will show you and expand upon it.
And the one that occurs in the back is not cured except for that which I recommend.
The patient should be extended on their back close to a wall with their feet stretched out so that the walls do not touch their feet.
And a straight staff should be placed nearby, fixed into the ground at the foot of the patient around their head, and another staff on the other side to elevate their feet at the other end of the staff.
The minister should hold the staff, and it should not be fixed in a pit; another minister should hold the other according to that shape.
And cover the patient's head with a cloth and tighten it around their forehead firmly; extend the end of the linen to the staff that should support their head, and tie it there; strengthen another linen across their buttocks, under their arms, and over their chest, and hold it by the feet; then adjust all the linens and tie them to the other staff that should support their feet.
And the minister should extend each linen to the end of the staff, and neither should the two staffs and linens at their respective locations where they are fixed be removed, but rather leave them as I have said before.
The doctor should place their hands firmly on the vertebra until it returns, or they should place a table under it and make sure that their feet are adjusted on the table until it returns.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table whose width or length is about three feet and carve into the wall near the patient's head a hole where the end of the table fits. Then place the tabletop on the vertebra and the doctor should place their feet on the other end of the table, pulling strongly until the vertebra is dislocated and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open the hole that is twisted with the hand; and it is that you squat in front of the patient's head at the end of two staffs, whose length should be one arm, and the distance between each staff should be one palm, and a groove should be made in each staff for the turning rod to pass through, and each rod should be firmly fixed on the ground so that it is not damaged.
And there should also be a round staff with the turning hole in both grooves of the two staffs.
And at the end of it is a groove in which the round rod with a length of one palm rotates; and the same applies to the other two staffs.
Then strengthen the linens that you have stretched in the patient's feet on the groove that should support their head, and the linens that you have stretched in their chest on the turning rod that should support their feet.
And you should also have each turn and a minister who turns the rod with their hand, while the doctor holds the vertebra as previously mentioned.
When the vertebra has returned and the location is covered, it is necessary to apply an adhesive plaster with egg white, and then stop it; then place a wooden splint on top of the plaster that is three fingers wide or more, and the length is the measurement taken at the site of the gibbosity, and above the edge of the vertebra.
And let the patient use the linen that we mentioned for nourishment until they are healed.
If there is still a weakness in the location at the end of the healing process, it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and liniment it for a long time with the administration of the table, which we have recommended; and a lead sheet has already been administered in that location.
And sometimes the imminence occurs in the lower part of the spine, called dislocation; and that bone is already inclined to such a condition so that it is not necessary to make an opening for it with this medicine, because great force may cause death.
Chapter thirty-one.
On treating a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle do not move but only dislocate, and it does not happen to those that happens to other joints due to small displacement and concavity.
The hip joint can be dislocated in four ways: it can be dislocated inside the pelvis, outside the pelvis, in front, or behind.
Most commonly, it is dislocated inside the pelvis, and rarely in front or behind.
The sign of the dislocation in the pelvis is when, when you align the healthy person's hip with the patient's, the healthy one appears longer and its tip is more prominent than the patient's, and the patient cannot bend their leg. You should anoint the area with cauterized dressing, so that the head of the dislocated bone has already been reached.
The sign of the dislocation that occurs outside the pelvis is that the accidents of it are contrary to this accident.
The sign of the dislocation that occurs in front is that the thigh expands according to its natural shape, but it does not bend without pain at the tip, and if it is forced to move, it cannot go forward; the urine is retained and the dressing must be applied to it, and you should walk on your heels.
The sign of the dislocation that occurs behind is that the tip does not extend and cannot bend the front part where it bends the dressing, and the thigh is shorter than the other, and the dressing is softened, and you should reach for the head of the dislocated bone there.
But the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider whether the dislocation is old and ancient, in which case it has already become fixed in its position, in which case it is not a cure; therefore, it is not necessary to make an opening for it.
But in the case of a recent dislocation (one of the four types of dislocations), you should try to move the joint and extend it in both directions, left and right, because great force will return it and it does not need any other treatment.
If it does not return in this way, then you must prepare strong medicine and extend the thigh of the affected side from the lower part, or with your hands, or apply a ligament on top of the thigh below the tip; and another minister should extend their hand above it as far as possible to press down on the dislocated bone; and another minister should press strongly on the ligament near the root of the joint, and another minister should hold the end of the ligament firmly.
The extension of the limb or from the pelvis to the ankle, or from the back to the lower back.
And the extension should be done all at once while the patient is lifted off the ground and remains hanging.
This type of extension is common to all four types.
Therefore, if the dislocation returns with what has been said, it will be good; and if not, it is necessary for all types to follow the instructions for their own treatment.
In the reduction of the dislocated part, when it is within the joints, it is necessary that the patient lies on their side; then place cushions under the affected area where the head of the dislocation is located and the site to be reduced, extending the limb in the direction opposite the dislocation to the same side of the clavicle.
And take another strong servant and grasp the patient's arms, and forcefully pull the affected joint in the opposite direction to extend it wide apart, because that will return it to its place.
And this type of treatment is for light cure, in which the limb can be reduced more easily than by other types.
But if the patient resists and refuses to cooperate with this type of treatment, it is necessary that both of their feet be bound tightly, loosely, around both the ankles and wrists.
And let each side be of four fingers in length, and the affected area should be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And suspend the patient over the head of the post (or bed), so that they are in the highest position possible; and let their feet be two arm-lengths away from the ground.
And command a strong young man to grasp the head of the dislocated joint, and suspend another young man from the patient. The third young man should push on the affected joint with force, because the joint will return to its place quickly.
But in the reduction of a dislocation outside the joint, it is necessary for the patient to lie face down as previously recommended; and tie the affected area tightly with ropes around the chest and feet.
And two posts should be placed, one at the feet and the other at the head; and posts adjusted to the middle of the beam fixed above on which damp cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not damaged by the post being between their thighs, so that the extension at the affected joint is not restricted.
And each servant should extend their part, and the doctor with his hand guides the reduction according to the art (or technique).
And when the dislocation is at the front joint, it should be extended slightly and in accordance with this narrative on the beam (or post). The doctor places his right hand on the affected limb, and with the other hand he compresses that area, and with that compression, he extends the affected arm to the opposite side of the hip.
And if the dislocation is at the back joint, it should not be extended to the opposite side, but raised above the ground; and it should be placed on a hard surface, as in the case where an ankle is dislocated outside the joint, as previously discussed about the position of the patient on two cushions, and his face should be placed down, and the limbs should be extended, as previously mentioned, which will not cause harm.
And it is necessary to apply compression with a table on the site where the joint is located.
So, as the reduction is complex, and the method of reducing it is not hidden from you (you will be extending the patient's legs), when you see them straightened out with no resistance, you will know that the limb, according to what is necessary, has been returned. Then you adjust the thighs and bind them tightly with bandages so that the affected area does not become swollen in any direction; and keep the patient resting for three or four days.
And loosen the bandage and the padding, and compare one leg with the other.
If you see them straightened out in their proper position, then you will know that the dislocation is already fixed, and at this point, let the patient get up.
If you see any signs of swelling, then return and treat that area, extend it according to your first structure, and let it rest for three days.
And loosen that area, and make it walk slowly on crutches for several days until it is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle can be dislocated in three ways: it is dislocated outside the joint, inside the joint, and more often behind the joint; but not in front of the joint.
The sign of a dislocation is that you command the patient to move their ankle, and then you will know that the ankle is dislocated.
The restoration of all three types of dislocations is that you straighten the patient's leg, so that they can extend their ankle if possible; place your hands on the sides of it, and hold their foot between your feet; and grasp the ankle with both hands and keep them apart from each other with force, and another servant extends his foot until the ankle returns to its place.
The thigh bone adheres to the hip without moving away.
Apply that and let the thigh bone adhere to the hip, and apply bandages for three or four days, then remove them.
Do not make the patient walk for several days until he is healed and comfortable.
If the reduction cannot be accomplished, then administer strong extension with ligaments as mentioned in the treatment of the thigh.
Chapter XXXIII.
Treatment for the Dislocation of the Ankle.
The ankle is sometimes moved slightly out of place by accident, and at other times it is dislocated due to trauma, and the dislocation occurs either inside the joint or outside.
The sign of ankle dislocation is that the ankle appears swollen around the area where it is dislocated.
However, the treatment for a slight displacement is easy reduction, and it should be extended with ease and then returned to its normal position.
If the dislocation occurs due to trauma, one must first immobilize the patient correctly, and have a strong and capable assistant from behind the feet in the correct position.
Place your right hand on the upper part of the patient's foot and your left hand on the lower part of the foot near the heel in the location of the cavity.
Pull the foot towards you with your right hand and push it away from you twice with your left hand, extending it, then push on the front of the shinbone with your left hand while pushing backward on the heel with your right hand to force the bones into their proper alignment.
Do this as many times as necessary until you achieve the desired result.
Push the shinbone toward the hip one last time, and you are at the joint.
Therefore, if it returns in one or two days using this method and the foot appears swollen, that is good; otherwise, repeat the operation as it will return.
If the reduction of it is forbidden as prescribed by what you have recommended, then place the patient on their back on the ground, stretch out a flat stone on the ground, and let them lower themselves onto it so that they rest between their thighs, and you have already covered them with cloth to prevent discomfort for the patient.
And place the minister in charge of it with their knees, and another minister should extend the foot with their hands or secure it with ropes around the neck of the foot; and each minister should extend that part as far as they can, and the stone should remain between the patient's thighs to hold it in place so that the body is not torn apart at the sides.
And the doctor should compress the dislocation with their hands, and another minister should hold the healthy part at the sides, as the dislocation will return easily.
And when the dislocation returns and you have confirmed the health of the reduction of it, then stop applying the plaster and remove it firmly, and tie the leg to the bed.
And be careful or protect yourself from the nerves that are located behind the ankle, so that the ropes around it are not tight and do not hurt it too much.
And keep it like that for two or three days; if the ropes become softened, remove them.
Then untie it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for 40 days. But if it is necessary to go in front of that space, it is not certain that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and approach what does not receive treatment.
And if it turns into a cauterized abscess, you must administer the prescribed treatment in the location of cure and dressing mentioned in our narrative until it heals over.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the cure of dislocation of the fingers of the feet.
It must be compressed gently without much extension; and that is not difficult, it is easy to do so.
But if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, it must be compressed with the patient's foot on a level surface such as the ground or a table, and remain like that as if walking.
And you stand by the patient, and place your foot on top of what supports the joints, and press down on it with your foot using force until it returns, and make sure that it remains compressed and does not appear again in the elevated area.
And place the patient's foot on a table that holds the entire foot, which has two arms, and strengthen it with a suitable and secure structure for three days; then remove it and allow it to rest from walking until it is strengthened and the reduction is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
On the types of dislocation caused by a wound, fracture, or any combination thereof.
When one of those occurs and you engage in the cure and restoration of it, many times death occurs after several days.
And therefore, in the treatment of it, do not presume without being knowledgeable and experienced in the art, long practice, mercy, compassion, patience, humility, and modesty; use medicines that dissolve cauterized abscesses only, and leave the patient to divine judgment, but help the one you hope will be saved from destruction, with relief from pain, and in which you see some hope. And therefore, strive to make the reduction at the beginning of the cause before abscesses occur.
And therefore, if the member returns as desired, then administer the treatment that dissolves cauterized abscesses and heals the wound with what is appropriate for dry dressings.
And if the dislocation is accompanied by fracture and separate bone fragments, you should perform the setting of them, and do what we said in simple fractures as mentioned in those places.
Keep and avoid what we have mentioned in the paths of fear as preceded by my teachings; for that is better for you, it will please your Creator, and more persistent to your glory, and healthier to your true body in your time. Thus ends the words of Al-Zahrawi, may God be pleased with him. Amen.
